<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Amazon rainforest</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Rainforest in South America</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"The Amazon" and "Amazonia" redirect here. For the river, see <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Amazon_(disambiguation)" title="Amazon (disambiguation)">Amazon</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Amazonia_(disambiguation)" title="Amazonia (disambiguation)">Amazonia (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

(2,100,000 sq mi)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Amazon rainforest</b>, <b>Amazon jungle</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> or <b>Amazonia</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">moist broadleaf</a> <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_biome" title="Amazon biome">Amazon biome</a> that covers most of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a> of South America. This basin encompasses 7,000,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,700,000 sq mi), of which 5,500,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,100,000 sq mi) are covered by the <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a>. This region includes territory belonging to nine nations and 3,344 formally acknowledged <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_territory_(Brazil)" title="Indigenous territory (Brazil)">indigenous territories</a>.
</p><p>The majority of the forest is contained <a href="/wiki/Amaz%C3%B4nia_Legal" title="Amazônia Legal">within Brazil</a>, with 60% of the rainforest, followed by <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_Amazonia" title="Peruvian Amazonia">Peru</a> with 13%, <a href="/wiki/Amazon_natural_region" title="Amazon natural region">Colombia</a> with 10%, and with minor amounts in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>. Four nations have "<a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Amazonas_(disambiguation)" title="Amazonas (disambiguation)">Amazonas</a>" as the name of one of their first-level administrative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regions" title="Regions">regions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> uses the name "<a href="/wiki/Guiana_Amazonian_Park" title="Guiana Amazonian Park">Guiana Amazonian Park</a>" for its rainforest protected area. The Amazon represents over half of the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>'s remaining rainforests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> and comprises the largest and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiverse" title="Biodiverse">biodiverse</a> tract of <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> rainforest in the world, with an estimated 390 billion individual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trees" title="Trees">trees</a> divided into 16,000 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurekalert.org_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-eurekalert.org-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than 30 million people of 350 different ethnic groups live in the Amazon, which are subdivided into 9 different national political systems and 3,344 formally acknowledged <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_territory_(Brazil)" title="Indigenous territory (Brazil)">indigenous territories</a>. Indigenous peoples make up 9% of the total population with 60 of the groups remaining largely isolated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The name <i>Amazon</i> is said to arise from a war <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Orellana" title="Francisco de Orellana">Francisco de Orellana</a> fought with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tapuyas" title="Tapuyas">Tapuyas</a> and other tribes. The women of the tribe fought alongside the men, as was their custom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor1898_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1898-6">[5]</a></sup> Orellana derived the name <i>Amazonas</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Amazons" title="Amazons">Amazons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, described by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor1898_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1898-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_America#Amazon" title="History of South America">History of South America § Amazon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River#History" title="Amazon River">Amazon River § History</a></div>
<p>In the Amazonas, there has been fighting and wars between the neighboring tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Jivaroan_peoples" title="Jivaroan peoples">Jivaro</a>. Several tribes of the Jivaroan group, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuar_people" title="Shuar people">Shuar</a>, practised <a href="/wiki/Headhunting" title="Headhunting">headhunting</a> for trophies and <a href="/wiki/Shrunken_head" title="Shrunken head">headshrinking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> The accounts of missionaries to the area in the borderlands between Brazil and Venezuela have recounted constant infighting in the <a href="/wiki/Yanomami" title="Yanomami">Yanomami</a> tribes. More than a third of the Yanomamo males, on average, died from warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rubber_boom" title="Amazon rubber boom">Amazon rubber boom</a> it is estimated that diseases brought by immigrants, such as <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, killed 40,000 native Amazonians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span></h3>
<p>Nine countries share the Amazon basin—most of the rainforest, 58.4%, is contained within the borders of Brazil. The other eight countries include Peru with 12.8%, Bolivia with 7.7%, Colombia with 7.1%, Venezuela with 6.1%, Guyana with 3.1%, Suriname with 2.5%, French Guiana with 1.4%, and Ecuador with 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Segamazonia_Coca-Castro_201301_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Segamazonia_Coca-Castro_201301-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural">Natural</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walking_tree.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Walking_tree.jpg/220px-Walking_tree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Walking_tree.jpg/330px-Walking_tree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Walking_tree.jpg/440px-Walking_tree.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walking_tree.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amazon rainforest in Colombia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2632" data-file-width="4123" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/220px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/330px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/440px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of the Amazon rainforest, near <a href="/wiki/Manaus" title="Manaus">Manaus</a></div></div></div>
<p>The rainforest likely formed during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> era (from 56 million years to 33.9 million years ago). It appeared following a global reduction of tropical temperatures when the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> had widened sufficiently to provide a warm, moist climate to the Amazon basin. The rainforest has been in existence for at least 55 million years, and most of the region remained free of <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a>-type <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a> at least until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_ice_age" title="Current ice age">current ice age</a> when the climate was drier and savanna more widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morley_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-morley-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptors359_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ptors359-12">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and the wetter climate may have allowed the tropical rainforest to spread out across the continent. From 66 to 34 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">Mya</a>, the rainforest extended as far south as <a href="/wiki/45th_parallel_south" title="45th parallel south">45°</a>. Climate fluctuations during the last 34 million years have allowed savanna regions to expand into the tropics. During the <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, for example, the rainforest spanned a relatively narrow band. It expanded again during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene" title="Middle Miocene">Middle Miocene</a>, then retracted to a mostly inland formation at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> However, the rainforest still managed to thrive during these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_periods" title="Glacial periods">glacial periods</a>, allowing for the survival and evolution of a broad diversity of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yadvinder_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-yadvinder-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Campo12Foto_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campo12Foto_2.JPG/220px-Campo12Foto_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campo12Foto_2.JPG/330px-Campo12Foto_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Campo12Foto_2.JPG/440px-Campo12Foto_2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Campo12Foto_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of the Amazon rainforest</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Eocene" title="Mid-Eocene">mid-Eocene</a>, it is believed that the drainage basin of the Amazon was split along the middle of the continent by the <a href="/wiki/P%C3%BArus_Arch" title="Púrus Arch">Púrus Arch</a>. Water on the eastern side flowed toward the Atlantic, while to the west water flowed toward the Pacific across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazonas_Basin" title="Amazonas Basin">Amazonas Basin</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> Mountains rose, however, a large basin was created that enclosed a lake; now known as the <a href="/wiki/Solim%C3%B5es_River" title="Solimões River">Solimões Basin</a>. Within the last 5–10 million years, this accumulating water broke through the Púrus Arch, joining the easterly flow toward the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is evidence that there have been significant changes in the Amazon rainforest <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a> over the last 21,000 years through the last glacial maximum (LGM) and subsequent deglaciation. Analyses of sediment deposits from Amazon basin paleolakes and the Amazon Fan indicate that rainfall in the basin during the LGM was lower than for the present, and this was almost certainly associated with reduced moist tropical vegetation cover in the basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colinvaux1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colinvaux1-17">[16]</a></sup> In present day, the Amazon receives approximately 9 feet of rainfall annually. There is a debate, however, over how extensive this reduction was. Some scientists argue that the rainforest was reduced to small, isolated <a href="/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology)" title="Refugium (population biology)">refugia</a> separated by open forest and grassland;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanDerHammen_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanDerHammen-18">[17]</a></sup> other scientists argue that the rainforest remained largely intact but extended less far to the north, south, and east than is seen today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colinvaux2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colinvaux2-19">[18]</a></sup> This debate has proved difficult to resolve because the practical limitations of working in the rainforest mean that data sampling is biased away from the center of the Amazon basin, and both explanations are reasonably well supported by the available data.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sahara_Desert_dust_windblown_to_the_Amazon">Sahara Desert dust windblown to the Amazon</span></h4>
<p>More than 56% of the dust fertilizing the Amazon rainforest comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bod%C3%A9l%C3%A9_depression" title="Bodélé depression">Bodélé depression</a> in Northern Chad in the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert. The dust contains <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, important for plant growth. The yearly Sahara dust replaces the equivalent amount of phosphorus washed away yearly in Amazon soil from rains and floods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>NASA's <a href="/wiki/CALIPSO" title="CALIPSO">CALIPSO</a> satellite has measured the amount of dust transported by wind from the Sahara to the Amazon: an average of 182 million tons of dust are windblown out of the Sahara each year, at 15 degrees west longitude, across 2,600 km (1,600 mi) over the Atlantic Ocean (some dust falls into the Atlantic), then at 35 degrees West longitude at the eastern coast of South America, 27.7 million tons (15%) of dust fall over the Amazon basin (22 million tons of it consisting of phosphorus), 132 million tons of dust remain in the air, 43 million tons of dust are windblown and falls on the Caribbean Sea, past 75 degrees west longitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>CALIPSO uses a laser range finder to scan the Earth's atmosphere for the vertical distribution of dust and other aerosols. CALIPSO regularly tracks the Sahara-Amazon dust plume. CALIPSO has measured variations in the dust amounts transported – an 86 percent drop between the highest amount of dust transported in 2007 and the lowest in 2011.
</p><p>A possibility causing the variation is the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>, a strip of semi-arid land on the southern border of the Sahara. When rain amounts in the Sahel are higher, the volume of dust is lower. The higher rainfall could make more vegetation grow in the Sahel, leaving less sand exposed to winds to blow away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amazon phosphorus also comes as smoke due to biomass burning in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_activity">Human activity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg/220px-Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg/330px-Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg/440px-Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skyline_Parcial_de_Manaus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Manaus" title="Manaus">Manaus</a>, with 2.2 million inhabitants, is the largest city in the Amazon basin</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1176" data-file-width="1768" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg/220px-Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg/330px-Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg/440px-Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inneres_eines_Schabono_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Yanomami" title="Yanomami">Yanomami</a> are a group of approximately 32,000 indigenous people who live in the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="699" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/220px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/330px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/440px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncontacted_tribe" title="Uncontacted tribe">uncontacted tribe</a> encountered in the Brazilian state of <a href="/wiki/Acre_(state)" title="Acre (state)">Acre</a> in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Based on <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> evidence from an excavation at <a href="/wiki/Caverna_da_Pedra_Pintada" title="Caverna da Pedra Pintada">Caverna da Pedra Pintada</a>, human inhabitants first settled in the Amazon region at least 11,200 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Subsequent development led to late-prehistoric settlements along the periphery of the forest by AD 1250, which induced alterations in the <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">forest cover</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>For a long time, it was thought that the Amazon rainforest was never more than sparsely populated, as it was impossible to sustain a large population through <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> given the poor soil. Archeologist <a href="/wiki/Betty_Meggers" title="Betty Meggers">Betty Meggers</a> was a prominent proponent of this idea, as described in her book <i>Amazonia: Man and Culture in a Counterfeit Paradise</i>. She claimed that a population density of 0.2 inhabitants per square kilometre (0.52/sq mi) is the maximum that can be sustained in the rainforest through hunting, with agriculture needed to host a larger population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> However, recent <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a> findings have suggested that the region was actually densely populated. Some 5 million people may have lived in the Amazon region in AD 1500, divided between dense coastal settlements, such as that at <a href="/wiki/Marajoara_culture" title="Marajoara culture">Marajó</a>, and inland dwellers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-park_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-park-29">[28]</a></sup> By 1900, the population had fallen to 1 million and by the early 1980s it was less than 200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-park_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-park-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first European to travel the length of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a> was <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Orellana" title="Francisco de Orellana">Francisco de Orellana</a> in 1542.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The BBC's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unnatural_Histories_(TV_series)" title="Unnatural Histories (TV series)">Unnatural Histories</a></i> presents evidence that Orellana, rather than exaggerating his claims as previously thought, was correct in his observations that a complex civilization was flourishing along the Amazon in the 1540s. It is believed that civilization was later devastated by the spread of diseases from Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-31">[30]</a></sup> This civilization was investigated by the British explorer <a href="/wiki/Percy_Fawcett" title="Percy Fawcett">Percy Fawcett</a> in the early twentieth century.  The results of his expeditions were inconclusive and he disappeared mysteriously on his last trip.  His name for this lost civilization was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_of_Z" title="City of Z">City of Z</a>.
</p><p>Since the 1970s, numerous <a href="/wiki/Geoglyph" title="Geoglyph">geoglyphs</a> have been discovered on deforested land dating between AD 1–1250, furthering claims about <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian</a> civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Ondemar Dias is accredited with first discovering the geoglyphs in 1977, and Alceu Ranzi is credited with furthering their discovery after flying over <a href="/wiki/Acre_(state)" title="Acre (state)">Acre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> The BBC's <i>Unnatural Histories</i> presented evidence that the Amazon rainforest, rather than being a pristine <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a>, has been shaped by man for at least 11,000 years through practices such as <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">forest gardening</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_preta" title="Terra preta">terra preta</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-31">[30]</a></sup> Terra preta is found over large areas in the Amazon forest; and is now widely accepted as a product of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Soil_management" title="Soil management">soil management</a>. The development of this fertile soil allowed agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Silviculture" title="Silviculture">silviculture</a> in the previously hostile environment; meaning that large portions of the Amazon rainforest are probably the result of centuries of human management, rather than naturally occurring as has previously been supposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> In the region of the <a href="/wiki/Xingu_peoples" title="Xingu peoples">Xingu</a> tribe, remains of some of these large settlements in the middle of the Amazon forest were found in 2003 by Michael Heckenberger and colleagues of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a>. Among those were evidence of roads, bridges and large plazas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Biodiversity.2C_flora_and_fauna"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity,_flora_and_fauna">Biodiversity, flora and fauna</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_plants_of_Amazon_Rainforest_vegetation_of_Brazil" title="List of plants of Amazon Rainforest vegetation of Brazil">List of plants of Amazon Rainforest vegetation of Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazonian_manatee" title="Amazonian manatee">Amazonian manatee</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2944" data-file-width="2178" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg/170px-Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg/255px-Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg/340px-Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ara_macao_-_two_at_Lowry_Park_Zoo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scarlet_macaw" title="Scarlet macaw">Scarlet macaw</a>, which is indigenous to the American tropics.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1035" data-file-width="1250" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg/220px-Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg/330px-Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg/440px-Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Waxy_Monkey_Frog.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> in the Amazon rainforest threatens many species of tree frogs, which are very sensitive to environmental changes (pictured: <a href="/wiki/Phyllomedusa_bicolor" title="Phyllomedusa bicolor">giant leaf frog</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bauhinia_guianensis,_mature_liana_(9340889588).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg/220px-Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg/330px-Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg/440px-Bauhinia_guianensis%2C_mature_liana_%289340889588%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bauhinia_guianensis,_mature_liana_(9340889588).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A giant, bundled <a href="/wiki/Liana" title="Liana">liana</a> in western Brazil</div></div></div>
<p>Wet tropical forests are the most species-rich <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a>, and tropical forests in the Americas are consistently more species rich than the wet forests in Africa and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_2001_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner_2001-37">[36]</a></sup> As the largest tract of tropical rainforest in the Americas, the Amazonian rainforests have unparalleled <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. One in ten known species in the world lives in the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> This constitutes the largest collection of living <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> in the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a>.
</p><p>The region is home to about 2.5 million insect <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> tens of thousands of plants, and some 2,000 birds and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. To date, at least 40,000 plant species, 2,200 <a href="/wiki/Neotropical_fish" title="Neotropical fish">fishes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AlbertReis2011_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-AlbertReis2011-40">[39]</a></sup> 1,294 birds, 427 mammals, 428 amphibians, and 378 reptiles have been scientifically classified in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Da_Silva_2005_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Da_Silva_2005-41">[40]</a></sup> One in five of all bird species are found in the Amazon rainforest, and one in five of the fish species live in Amazonian rivers and streams. Scientists have described between 96,660 and 128,843 <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> species in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewinsohn_2005_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewinsohn_2005-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biodiversity of plant species is the highest on Earth with one 2001 study finding a quarter square kilometer (62 acres) of Ecuadorian rainforest supports more than 1,100 tree species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> A study in 1999 found one square kilometer (247 acres) of Amazon rainforest can contain about 90,790 tonnes of living plants. The average plant biomass is estimated at 356 ± 47 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> To date, an estimated 438,000 species of plants of economic and social interest have been registered in the region with many more remaining to be discovered or catalogued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> The total number of <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a> species in the region is estimated at 16,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eurekalert.org_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-eurekalert.org-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The green leaf area of plants and trees in the rainforest varies by about 25% as a result of seasonal changes. Leaves expand during the dry season when sunlight is at a maximum, then undergo abscission in the cloudy wet season. These changes provide a balance of carbon between photosynthesis and respiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rainforest contains several species that can pose a hazard. Among the largest predatory creatures are the <a href="/wiki/Black_caiman" title="Black caiman">black caiman</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_American_jaguar" title="South American jaguar">jaguar</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_American_cougar" title="South American cougar">cougar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anaconda" title="Anaconda">anaconda</a>. In the river, <a href="/wiki/Electric_eel" title="Electric eel">electric eels</a> can produce an electric shock that can stun or kill, while <a href="/wiki/Piranha" title="Piranha">piranha</a> are known to bite and injure humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Various species of <a href="/wiki/Poison_dart_frog" title="Poison dart frog">poison dart frogs</a> secrete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipophilic" title="Lipophilic">lipophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloid</a> toxins through their flesh. There are also numerous parasites and disease vectors. <a href="/wiki/Vampire_bat" title="Vampire bat">Vampire bats</a> dwell in the rainforest and can spread the <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> virus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">Malaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a> can also be contracted in the Amazon region.
</p><p>The biodiversity in the Amazon is becoming increasingly threatened, primarily by habitat loss from deforestation as well as increased frequency of fires. Over 90% of Amazonian plant and vertebrate species (13,000-14,000 in total) may have been impacted to some degree by fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deforestation">Deforestation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg/220px-2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg/330px-2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg/440px-2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2021_Top_ten_countries_for_tropical_primary_forest_loss_-_World_Resources_Institute.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Home to much of the Amazon rainforest, Brazil's tropical <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">primary (old-growth)</a> forest loss greatly exceeds that of other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRI_20220428_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-WRI_20220428-50">[49]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation,_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg/220px-20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg/330px-20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg/440px-20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation%2C_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20220910_Amazon_deforestation_and_degradation,_by_country_-_Amazon_Watch.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overall, 20% of the Amazon rainforest has been "transformed" (deforested) and another 6% has been "highly degraded", causing Amazon Watch to warn that the Amazonia is in the midst of a tipping point crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmazonWatch_202209_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmazonWatch_202209-51">[50]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> is the conversion of forested areas to non-forested areas. The main sources of deforestation in the Amazon are human settlement and the development of the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> In 2018, about 17% of the Amazon rainforest was already destroyed. Research suggests that upon reaching about 20–25% (hence 3–8% more), the <a href="/wiki/Tipping_points_in_the_climate_system" title="Tipping points in the climate system">tipping point</a> to flip it into a non-forest ecosystem – degraded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannah</a> – (in eastern, southern and central Amazonia) will be reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to the early 1960s, access to the forest's interior was highly restricted, and the forest remained basically intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-55">[54]</a></sup> Farms established during the 1960s were based on crop cultivation and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a> method. However, the colonists were unable to manage their fields and the crops because of the loss of <a href="/wiki/Soil_fertility" title="Soil fertility">soil fertility</a> and weed invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watkins_griffiths_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-watkins_griffiths-56">[55]</a></sup> The soils in the Amazon are productive for just a short period of time, so farmers are constantly moving to new areas and clearing more land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watkins_griffiths_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-watkins_griffiths-56">[55]</a></sup> These farming practices led to deforestation and caused extensive environmental damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Deforestation is considerable, and areas cleared of forest are visible to the naked eye from outer space.
</p><p>In the 1970s, construction began on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Amazonian_highway" title="Trans-Amazonian highway">Trans-Amazonian highway</a>. This highway represented a major threat to the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> The highway still has not been completed, limiting the environmental damage.
</p><p>Between 1991 and 2000, the total area of forest lost in the Amazon rose from 415,000 to 587,000 km<sup>2</sup> (160,000 to 227,000 sq mi), with most of the lost forest becoming pasture for cattle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIFOR2004_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIFOR2004-59">[58]</a></sup> Seventy percent of formerly forested land in the Amazon, and 91% of land deforested since 1970, have been used for livestock <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">pasture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Currently, Brazil is the largest global producer of <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a>. New research however, conducted by Leydimere Oliveira et al., has shown that the more rainforest is logged in the Amazon, the less precipitation reaches the area and so the lower the yield per hectare becomes. So despite the popular perception, there has been no economical advantage for Brazil from logging rainforest zones and converting these to pastoral fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1772" data-file-width="2598" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/220px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/330px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg/440px-Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_protesters_from_Vale_do_Javar%C3%AD_in_Bel%C3%A9m_2009_1530FP8886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indigenous protesters from <a href="/wiki/Vale_do_Javari" title="Vale do Javari">Vale do Javari</a></div></div></div>
<p>The needs of soy farmers have been used to justify many of the controversial transportation projects that are currently developing in the Amazon. The first two highways successfully opened up the rainforest and led to increased settlement and deforestation. The mean annual deforestation rate from 2000 to 2005 (22,392 km<sup>2</sup> or 8,646 sq mi per year) was 18% higher than in the previous five years (19,018 km<sup>2</sup> or 7,343 sq mi per year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Although deforestation declined significantly in the Brazilian Amazon between 2004 and 2014, there has been an increase to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Brazil's President, Jair Bolsonaro, has supported the relaxation of regulations placed on agricultural land. He has used his time in office to allow for more deforestation and more exploitation of the Amazon's rich natural resources.
</p><p>Since the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> reservoirs in the Amazon rainforest, oil drilling activity has steadily increased, peaking in the Western Amazon in the 1970s and ushering another drilling boom in the 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Oil companies have to set up their operations by opening new roads through the forests, which often contributes to deforestation in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union%E2%80%93Mercosur_free_trade_agreement" title="European Union–Mercosur free trade agreement">European Union–Mercosur free trade agreement</a>, which would form one of the world's largest free trade areas, has been denounced by environmental activists and indigenous rights campaigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> The fear is that the deal could lead to more deforestation of the Amazon rainforest as it expands market access to Brazilian beef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a November 2021 report by Brazil's <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Space_Research" title="National Institute for Space Research">INPE</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite#Environmental_monitoring" title="Earth observation satellite">satellite data</a>, deforestation has increased by 22% over 2020 and is at its highest level since 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2019_fires">2019 fires</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2019_Amazon_rainforest_wildfires" title="2019 Amazon rainforest wildfires">2019 Amazon rainforest wildfires</a></div>
<p>There have been 72,843 fires in Brazil in 2019, with more than half within the Amazon region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>  In August 2019 there were a record number of fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Brazil" title="Deforestation in Brazil">Deforestation in the Brazilian Amazon</a> rose more than 88% in June 2019 compared with the same month in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</div></li>
</ul><p>The increased area of fire-impacted forest coincided with a relaxation of environmental regulations from the Brazilian government. Notably, before those regulations were put in place in 2008 the fire-impacted area was also larger compared to the regulation period of 2009–2018. As these fire continue to move closer to the heart of the Amazon Basin, their impact on biodiversity will only increase in scale, as the cumulative fire-impacted area is correlated with the number of species impacted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-49">[48]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_and_climate_change">Conservation and climate change</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_and_climate_change" title="Deforestation and climate change">Deforestation and climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaviotas" title="Gaviotas">Gaviotas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(5).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg/230px-Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg/345px-Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg/460px-Amazon_CIAT_%285%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(5).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amazon rainforest</div></div></div>
<p>Environmentalists are concerned about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_biodiversity" title="Loss of biodiversity">loss of biodiversity</a> that will result from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_destruction" title="Forest destruction">destruction of the forest</a>, and also about the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">release of the carbon</a> contained within the vegetation, which could accelerate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Amazonian evergreen forests account for about 10% of the world's terrestrial primary productivity and 10% of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon stores</a> in ecosystems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melillo_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melillo-76">[75]</a></sup> – of the order of 1.1 × 10<sup>11</sup> metric tonnes of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tian_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tian-77">[76]</a></sup> Amazonian forests are estimated to have accumulated 0.62 ± 0.37 tons of carbon per hectare per year between 1975 and 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tian_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tian-77">[76]</a></sup> In 2021 it was reported that the Amazon for the first time emitted more greenhouse gases than it absorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Though often referenced as producing more than a quarter of the Earth's oxygen, this often stated, but misused statistic actually refers to oxygen turnover.  The net contribution of the ecosystem is approximately zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>One <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">computer model</a> of future <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> shows that the Amazon rainforest could become unsustainable under conditions of severely reduced rainfall and increased temperatures, leading to an almost complete loss of rainforest cover in the basin by 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radford_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radford-81">[80]</a></sup> However, simulations of Amazon basin climate change across many different models are not consistent in their estimation of any rainfall response, ranging from weak increases to strong decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-82">[81]</a></sup> The result indicates that the rainforest could be threatened through the 21st century by climate change in addition to deforestation.
</p><p>In 1989, environmentalist C.M. Peters and two colleagues stated there is economic as well as biological incentive to protecting the rainforest. One hectare in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peruvian_Amazon" title="Peruvian Amazon">Peruvian Amazon</a> has been calculated to have a value of $6820 if <a href="/wiki/Intact_forest_landscape" title="Intact forest landscape">intact forest</a> is sustainably harvested for fruits, latex, and timber; $1000 if clear-cut for commercial timber (not sustainably harvested); or $148 if used as cattle pasture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to WWF, <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a> could help the Amazon to reduce deforestation and climate change. Ecotourism is currently still little practiced in the Amazon, partly due to lack of information about places where implementation is possible. Ecotourism is a sector that can also be taken up by the Indigenous community in the Amazon as a source of income and revenue. An ecotourism project in the Brazilian-section of the Amazon rainforest had been under consideration by Brazil's State Secretary for the Environment and Sustainable Development in 2009, along the <a href="/wiki/Aripuan%C3%A3_River" title="Aripuanã River">Aripuanã river</a>, in the Aripuanã Sustainable Development Reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Also, some community-based ecotourism exists in the <a href="/wiki/Mamirau%C3%A1_Sustainable_Development_Reserve" title="Mamirauá Sustainable Development Reserve">Mamirauá Sustainable Development Reserve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecotourism_in_Peru" title="Ecotourism in Peru">Ecotourism is also practiced in the Peruvian-section of the Amazon rainforest</a>. A few ecolodges are for instance present between Cusco and Madre de Dios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="820" data-file-width="3380" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/440px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/660px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png/880px-Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isolierte-V%C3%B6lker.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of <a href="/wiki/Uncontacted_peoples" title="Uncontacted peoples">uncontacted tribes</a>, around the start of the 21st century</div></div></div>
<p>As indigenous territories continue to be destroyed by deforestation and <a href="/wiki/Ecocide" title="Ecocide">ecocide</a>, such as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peruvian_Amazon" title="Peruvian Amazon">Peruvian Amazon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dean_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dean-87">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a>' rainforest communities continue to disappear, while others, like the <a href="/wiki/Urarina" title="Urarina">Urarina</a> continue to struggle to fight for their cultural survival and the fate of their forested territories. Meanwhile, the relationship between non-human primates in the subsistence and symbolism of indigenous lowland South American peoples has gained increased attention, as have ethno-biology and <a href="/wiki/Community-based_conservation" title="Community-based conservation">community-based conservation</a> efforts.
</p><p>From 2002 to 2006, the conserved land in the Amazon rainforest almost tripled and deforestation rates dropped up to 60%. About 1,000,000 km<sup>2</sup> (250,000,000 acres) have been put onto some sort of conservation, which adds up to a current amount of 1,730,000 km<sup>2</sup> (430,000,000 acres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2019, the <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuadorian</a> court stopped oil exploration activities in 180,000 hectares (440,000 acres) of the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2019, the Ecuadorian court forbade the government to sell territory with forests to oil companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2019, the US and Brazil agreed to promote private-sector development in the Amazon. They also pledged a $100m  biodiversity conservation fund for the Amazon led by the private sector. Brazil's foreign minister stated that opening the rainforest to economic development was the only way to protect it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" title="Anthropogenic emission of greenhouse gases broken down by sector for the year 2000."><img alt="Anthropogenic emission of greenhouse gases broken down by sector for the year 2000." data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/300px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/450px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/600px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 203.33333333333px"><div style="width: 203.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 201.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:September_Smoke_Over_the_Amazon_from_2005-2008.png" title="Aerosols over the Amazon each September for four burning seasons (2005 through 2008). </p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg" title="Aerial roots of red mangrove on an Amazonian river."><img alt="Aerial roots of red mangrove on an Amazonian river." data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg/400px-Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg/600px-Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg/800px-Roots_by_cesarpb.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Aerial roots of red mangrove on an Amazonian river.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 150.66666666667px"><div style="width: 150.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 148.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg" title="Climate change disturbances of rainforests.[91]"><img alt="Climate change disturbances of rainforests.[91]" data-file-height="1116" data-file-width="829" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg/223px-Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg/334px-Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg/446px-Climate_change_disturbances_of_rainforests_infographic.jpg 2x" width="149"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Climate change disturbances of rainforests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.3389/ffgc.2021.618401_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.3389/ffgc.2021.618401-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>A 2009 study found that a 4 °C rise (above pre-industrial levels) in global temperatures by 2100 would kill 85% of the Amazon rainforest while a temperature rise of 3 °C would kill some 75% of the Amazon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane,_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg/220px-Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg/330px-Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg/440px-Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane%2C_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parc_amazonien_de_Guyane,_une_balade_%C3%A0_Sa%C3%BCl.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guiana_Amazonian_Park" title="Guiana Amazonian Park">Guiana Amazonian Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a></div></div></div>
<p>A new study by an international team of environmental scientists in the Brazilian Amazon shows that protection of freshwater biodiversity can be increased by up to 600% through integrated freshwater-terrestrial planning 
.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> in the Amazon rainforest region has a negative impact on local climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> It was one of the main causes of the severe <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>  of 2014–2015 in Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> This is because the moisture from the forests is important to the rainfall in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>. Half of the rainfall in the Amazon area is produced by the forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Results of a 2021 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_review" title="Scientific review">scientific synthesis</a> indicate that, in terms of global warming,  the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a> with the Amazon rainforest is currently emitting more <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">it absorbs</a> overall. Climate change impacts and human activities in the area – mainly wildfires, current land-use and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">deforestation</a> – are causing a release of forcing agents that likely result in a net warming effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.3389/ffgc.2021.618401_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.3389/ffgc.2021.618401-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2022 the supreme court of Ecuador decided that "“under no circumstances can a project be carried out that generates excessive sacrifices to the collective rights of communities and nature.” It also required the government to respect the opinion of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> about different industrial projects on their land. Advocates of the decision argue that it will have consequences far beyond Ecuador. In general, ecosystems are in better shape when <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> own or manage the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remote_sensing">Remote sensing</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_monitoring" title="Environmental monitoring">Environmental monitoring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_management_system" title="Environmental management system">Environmental management system</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">Unmanned aerial vehicle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7200" data-file-width="5600" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg/220px-Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg/330px-Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg/440px-Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afternoon_Clouds_over_the_Amazon_Rainforest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This image reveals how the forest and the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> interact to create a uniform layer of "popcorn-shaped" <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_humilis_cloud" title="Cumulus humilis cloud">cumulus clouds</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remotely sensed</a> data is dramatically improving conservationists' knowledge of the Amazon basin. Given the objectivity and lowered costs of <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">satellite-based</a> land cover and -change analysis, it appears likely that remote sensing technology will be an integral part of assessing the extents, locations and damage of deforestation in the basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Furthermore, remote sensing is the best and perhaps only possible way to study the Amazon on a large scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of remote sensing for the conservation of the Amazon is also being used by the indigenous tribes of the basin to protect their tribal lands from commercial interests. Using handheld <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> devices and programs like <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a>, members of the Trio Tribe, who live in the rainforests of southern Suriname, map out their ancestral lands to help strengthen their territorial claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Currently, most tribes in the Amazon do not have clearly defined boundaries, making it easier for commercial ventures to target their territories.
</p><p>To accurately map the Amazon's biomass and subsequent carbon-related emissions, the classification of tree growth stages within different parts of the forest is crucial. In 2006, Tatiana Kuplich organized the trees of the Amazon into four categories: mature forest, regenerating forest [less than three years], regenerating forest [between three and five years of regrowth], and regenerating forest [eleven to eighteen years of continued development].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> The researcher used a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">synthetic aperture radar</a> (SAR) and <a href="/wiki/Thematic_Mapper" title="Thematic Mapper">Thematic Mapper</a> (TM) to accurately place the different portions of the Amazon into one of the four classifications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_early_21st-century_Amazon_droughts"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="Impact_of_early_21st-century_Amazon_droughts"></span><span id="Impact_of_early_21st_century_Amazon_droughts"></span><span class="vanchor-text">Impact of early 21st-century Amazon droughts</span></span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, parts of the Amazon basin experienced the worst drought in one hundred years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> and there were indications that 2006 may have been a second successive year of drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> A 2006 article in the UK newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> reported the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Research_Center" title="Woods Hole Research Center">Woods Hole Research Center</a> results, showing that the forest in its present form could survive only three years of drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Scientists at the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Amazonian_Research" title="National Institute of Amazonian Research">National Institute of Amazonian Research</a> argued in the article that this drought response, coupled with the effects of deforestation on regional climate, are pushing the rainforest towards a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tipping_point_(climatology)" title="Tipping point (climatology)">tipping point</a>" where it would irreversibly start to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> It concluded that the forest is on the brink of<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (August 2019)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> being turned into <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> or desert, with catastrophic consequences for the world's climate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A study published in <i>Nature Communications</i> in October 2020 found that about 40% of the Amazon rainforest is at risk of becoming a savanna-like ecosystem due to reduced rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> A study published in <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nature_climate_change&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nature climate change (page does not exist)">Nature climate change</a></i> provided direct empirical evidence that more than three-quarters of the Amazon rainforest has been losing resilience since the early 2000s, risking dieback with profound implications for biodiversity, carbon storage and climate change at a global scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a>, the combination of climate change and deforestation increases the drying effect of dead trees that fuels <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">forest fires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, the Amazon rainforest experienced another severe drought, in some ways more extreme than the 2005 drought. The affected region was approximately 3,000,000 km<sup>2</sup> (1,160,000 sq mi) of rainforest, compared with 1,900,000 km<sup>2</sup> (734,000 sq mi) in 2005. The 2010 drought had three epicenters where vegetation died off, whereas in 2005, the drought was focused on the southwestern part. The findings were published in the journal <i>Science</i>. In a typical year, the Amazon absorbs 1.5 gigatons of carbon dioxide; during 2005 instead 5 gigatons were released and in 2010 8 gigatons were released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Additional severe droughts occurred in 2010, 2015, and 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019 Brazil's protections of the Amazon rainforest were slashed, resulting in a severe loss of trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> According to Brazil's <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Space_Research" title="National Institute for Space Research">National Institute for Space Research</a> (INPE), deforestation in the Brazilian Amazon rose more than 50% in the first three months of 2020 compared to the same three-month period in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, a 17 percent rise was noted in the <a href="/wiki/2019_Amazon_rainforest_wildfires" title="2019 Amazon rainforest wildfires">Amazon wildfires</a>, marking the worst start to the fire season in a decade. The first 10 days of August 2020 witnessed 10,136 fires. An analysis of the government figures reflected 81 per cent increase in fires in federal reserves, in comparison with the same period in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> However, President <a href="/wiki/Jair_Bolsonaro" title="Jair Bolsonaro">Jair Bolsonaro</a> turned down the existence of fires, calling it a "lie", despite the data produced by his own government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Satellites in September recorded 32,017 hotspots in the world's largest rainforest, a 61% rise from the same month in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> In addition, October saw a huge surge in the number of hotspots in the forest (more than 17,000 fires are burning in the Amazon's rainforest) - with more than double the amount detected in the same month last year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amany%C3%A9" title="Amanyé">Amanyé</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Forest" title="Atlantic Forest">Atlantic Forest</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bandeirantes" title="Bandeirantes">Bandeirantes</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <i lang="pt">Floresta amazônica</i> or <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">Amazônia</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">Selva amazónica</i>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Amazonía</i></span>, or usually <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Amazonia</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Forêt amazonienne</i>; <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i lang="nl">Amazoneregenwoud</i>. In English, the names are sometimes capitalized further, as Amazon Rainforest, Amazon Forest, or Amazon Jungle.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
